{"Status": "Complete", "Paper Overview": {"type_of_reference": "JOUR", "Title": "Lessons from the First Multiply Imaged Supernova: Revised Strong-lensing Models for the Galaxy Cluster MACS J1149.5+2223", "authors": ["Zitrin, Adi"], "author_address": ["AA(Physics Department, Ben-Gurion University of the Negev, P.O. Box 653, Be'er-Sheva 84105, Israel)"], "journal_name": "The Astrophysical Journal", "volume": "919", "publication_year": "2021/09/1", "start_page": "54", "keywords": ["Dark matter", "Cosmology", "Navarro-Frenk-White profile", "Observational cosmology", "Galaxy clusters", "Gravitational lensing", "Strong gravitational lensing", "Cosmological parameters", "Hubble constant", "353", "343", "1091", "1146", "584", "670", "1643", "339", "758", "Astrophysics - Cosmology and Nongalactic Astrophysics"], "url": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2021ApJ...919...54Z", "abstract": "We present two grid-based lens models for the galaxy cluster MACS J1149.5+2223, concentrating on the properties of the first multiply imaged supernova Refsdal, which first appeared in this cluster as an Einstein cross (images S1-S4) around a cluster galaxy in late 2014, and about a year later as an additional image (SX) in a different image of the supernova (SN) host galaxy. One model is our updated light-traces-mass (LTM) strong-lensing model for this cluster, and the second is a parametric model (dPIEeNFW), generated using the same pipeline but with a different parameterization. Together these two models probe different possible solutions in a relatively self-consistent manner and can be used to examine systematic uncertainties and relevant differences between the two parameterizations. We obtain reasonably similar (agreeing to within \u22431\u03c3-3\u03c3, in most cases) time delays and magnification ratios, with respect to S1, from the two different methods, although the LTM predictions seem to be systematically shorter/smaller for some of the images. Most notably, the time delay [and 95% CI] between the Einstein cross (in particular, image S1), and SX, differs substantially between the parametric method (326 [300-359] days) and the LTM method (224 [198-306] days), which seems to underestimate the true reappearance time. The cause for this systematic difference is unclear at present. We speculate on its possible origin and note that a refined measurement of SN Refsdal's properties should help to more strongly discriminate between the two solutions, and thus between the two descriptions for the intrinsic shape of the underlying matter distribution. We also discuss the implications of our results for the Hubble constant.", "doi": "10.3847/1538-4357/ac0e32", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:2008.10642", "issn": "0004-637X", "number": "1"}, "ADS link": <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F919%2F1%2F54\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/919/1/54</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <p>We present two grid-based lens models for the galaxy cluster MACS J1149.5+2223, concentrating on the properties of the first multiply imaged supernova Refsdal, which first appeared in this cluster as an Einstein cross (images S1\u2013S4) around a cluster galaxy in late 2014, and about a year later as an additional image (SX) in a different image of the supernova (SN) host galaxy. One model is our updated light-traces-mass (LTM) strong-lensing model for this cluster, and the second is a parametric model (dPIEeNFW), generated using the same pipeline but with a different parameterization. Together these two models probe different possible solutions in a relatively self-consistent manner and can be used to examine systematic uncertainties and relevant differences between the two parameterizations. We obtain reasonably similar (agreeing to within \u22431<i>\u03c3</i>\u20133<i>\u03c3</i>, in most cases) time delays and magnification ratios, with respect to S1, from the two different methods, although the LTM predictions seem to be systematically shorter/smaller for some of the images. Most notably, the time delay [and 95% CI] between the Einstein cross (in particular, image S1), and SX, differs substantially between the parametric method (326 [300\u2013359] days) and the LTM method (224 [198\u2013306] days), which seems to underestimate the true reappearance time. The cause for this systematic difference is unclear at present. We speculate on its possible origin and note that a refined measurement of SN Refsdal's properties should help to more strongly discriminate between the two solutions, and thus between the two descriptions for the intrinsic shape of the underlying matter distribution. We also discuss the implications of our results for the Hubble constant.</p></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and class=\"linked-articles__wrapper\">\n\t\t<div class=\"linked-articles__prev\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/article/10.3847/1538-4357/ac0e35\"\n\t\t\t   class=\"linked-articles__btn event_read-more-articles\" class=\"linked-articles__btn event_read-more-articles\" data-event-action=\"Next Article\"><b>Next</b> article in issue<span\n\t\t\t\t\tclass=\"fa-icon fa-icon--right\"><svg aria-hidden=\"true\" 128.662c7.81 7.81 7.81 20.474 0 28.284L34.142 398.804C21.543 411.404 0 402.48 0 384.662z\"></path></svg></span></a>\n\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2021ApJ...919...54Z\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\"><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjac0e32s1\" name=\"apjac0e32s1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Strong-lensing (SL) by galaxies and clusters of galaxies has become an important astrophysical and cosmological tool (e.g., Kneib &amp; Natarajan <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib41\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib41\">2011</a>).</p><p>On galaxy scales, a relatively rare chance alignment between lensing galaxies and distant background sources results in multiple images of the source, and often, an approximate Einstein ring (e.g., Kochanek et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib42\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib42\">2001</a>; Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib7\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib7\">2006</a>). If the background, multiply imaged source is variable, a measurement of the time delay (TD) between different multiple images of the source becomes possible, allowing, in conjunction with a lens model, a measurement of the Hubble constant (e.g., Refsdal <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib65\">1964</a>). For instance, several strongly lensed quasars have been uncovered to date, some of them monitored over a long time span (e.g., Courbin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib15\">2011</a>; Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib74\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib74\">2013</a>; Tewes et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib75\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib75\">2013</a>) to derive measurements of the Hubble constant, reaching, for example, an updated value of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32ieqn1.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=wMb%2BRL%2FmbY6CerleSe1tX%2FEOl0A%3D\" alt=\"${H}_{0}={73.3}_{-1.8}^{+1.7}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{H}_{0}={73.3}_{-1.8}^{+1.7}</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup> (Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib81\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib81\">2020</a>). This value is in agreement with recent Cepheid calibration of type Ia supernova (SN) hosts (Riess et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib68\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib68\">2019</a>, cf. Tip of the Red Giant Branch calibration Freedman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib21\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib21\">2019</a>), but in tension with that derived by Planck measurements of the cosmic microwave background (Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib62\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib62\">2020</a>).</p><p>On galaxy-cluster scales, space imaging with Hubble has revealed that most massive clusters show an abundance of multiple images of lensed background sources near their centers (e.g., Richard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib67\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib67\">2010</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib90\">2015</a>; Cerny et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib12\">2018</a>; Paterno-Mahler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib61\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib61\">2018</a>; Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib2\">2020</a>), where the projected matter density is sufficiently high. Hand in hand, over the past 2\u20133 decades various methods for analyzing strong cluster lenses have been developed (e.g., Kneib et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib40\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib40\">1996</a>; Broadhurst et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib9\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib9\">2005</a>; Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib18\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib18\">2005</a>; Halkola et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib26\">2006</a>; Liesenborgs et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib44\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib44\">2006</a>; Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib33\">2007</a>). Understanding the systematic biases and differences between the various methods and between the different parameterizations, i.e., different representations of the underlying matter distribution, is crucial for advancing the resulting science from SL, and for understanding and reducing the errors on the outcome measurements. Among the science themes that will gain from an improved understanding of lensing systematics are, for example, the measurements of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> (e.g., Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib16\">2016</a>; Grillo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib24\">2018</a>) or of other cosmological parameters (e.g., Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib34\">2010</a>), the mapping of the distribution of matter in the lens (e.g., Limousin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib45\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib45\">2010</a>; Oguri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib59\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib59\">2012</a>; Jauzac et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib29\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib29\">2015</a>; Caminha et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib10\">2016</a>; Kawamata et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib35\">2016</a>; Monna et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib53\">2017</a>) and understanding its relation to the light distribution (e.g., Williams &amp; Saha <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib80\">2011</a>; Massey et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib48\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib48\">2015</a>; Chen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib13\">2020</a>), or the construction of luminosity functions for high-redshift lensed objects, to probe the reionization of the universe (e.g., Brada\u010d et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib8\">2012</a>; Atek et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib3\">2015</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib14\">2015</a>; Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib56\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib56\">2015</a>; McLeod et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib49\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib49\">2016</a>; Ishigaki et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib28\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib28\">2018</a>; Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib71\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib71\">2020</a>).</p><p>Various aspects of the systematics between different models have been investigated in recent years, such as the differences in terms of mass density or magnification, the effect of source redshifts on these, or the relation to cosmography (e.g., Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib69\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib69\">2015</a>; Johnson &amp; Sharon <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib31\">2016</a>; Meneghetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib51\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib51\">2017</a>; Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib1\">2017</a>; Birrer &amp; Treu <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib6\">2019</a>). However, much work remains and additional information is often needed to break some of the degeneracies inherent to a typical lensing analysis, such as the apparent degeneracy between the intrinsic ellipticity of matter and the contribution from external substructure or external shear (e.g., Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib90\">2015</a>, see also Kovner <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib43\">1987</a>; Narayan &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib54\">1996</a>; Keeton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib36\">1997</a>; Holder &amp; Schechter <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib27\">2003</a>; Schneider et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib52\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib52\">2006</a>). TDs and absolute magnifications (or in lack thereof, relative magnifications) hold the key to breaking some of these degeneracies, especially in models that only rely on multiple image positions as constraints, as is commonly the case.</p><p>About 5 yr ago, the first resolved multiply imaged SN was detected (Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib38\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib38\">2015</a>) in the field of the galaxy cluster MACS J1149.5+2223 (M1149 hereafter, Ebeling et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib19\">2007</a>), as an Einstein cross around a cluster galaxy close to the Brightest Cluster Galaxy (BCG). The SN appeared 50 yr after the original paper by Refsdal (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib65\">1964</a>), who proposed to use multiply imaged SNe to constrain the Hubble constant, and was named <em>SN Refsdal</em>. The spiral galaxy in which the SN exploded is itself lensed several times by the cluster (Smith et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib73\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib73\">2009</a>; Zitrin &amp; Broadhurst <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib88\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib88\">2009</a>), so that another image of the exploding SN was expected about a year post-discovery. This enabled useful blind tests of various lens modeling techniques. While most models broadly agreed with the actual reappearance, these tests (Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>) revealed some discrepancies and supplied invaluable input for lens modelers to examine their modeling schemes.</p><p>The galaxy cluster M1149 was detected thanks to its X-ray signal as part of the MAssive Cluster Survey (MACS; Ebeling et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib19\">2007</a>). The first lensing analysis of M1149 was published around the same time by Zitrin &amp; Broadhurst (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib88\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib88\">2009</a>), who proposed many of the gold multiple images in this cluster (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t2\">2</a>), and by Smith et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib73\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib73\">2009</a>), who also measured spectroscopically the redshift for some of these systems. Following its compelling features, M1149 was also observed in 16 bands as a high-magnification cluster in the Cluster Lensing And Supernova survey with Hubble treasury program (CLASH; Postman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib63\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib63\">2012</a>), and included in the subsequent ultra-deep Hubble Frontier Fields program (HFF; Lotz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib47\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib47\">2017</a>), and in the Grism Lens-Amplified Survey from Space (GLASS; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib77\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib77\">2015</a>). It has also been a part of extensive Atacama Large Millimeter/submillimeter Array (ALMA) programs, such as the ALMA Frontier Fields survey (PI: Bauer; e.g., Gonz\u00e1lez-L\u00f3pez et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib22\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib22\">2017</a>), or the ALMA Lensing Cluster Survey (ALCS; PI: Kohno). Following this rich coverage, including ground based spectroscopy (e.g., Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a> and references therein) many other lens models for the cluster have been published to date (e.g., Johnson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib32\">2014</a>; Rau et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib64\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib64\">2014</a>; Richard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib66\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib66\">2014</a>; Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib16\">2016</a>; Kawamata et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib35\">2016</a>, see also the HFF webpage).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjac0e32fn1\">\n               1\n            </a></sup>\n             \n         </p><p>We present here two new lens models for M1149\u2014a revised light-traces-mass (LTM) model and a grid-based parametric model\u2014and list the expected properties of SN Refsdal, namely, the TDs and magnification ratios between the different images. Over the past decade our LTM model for M1149, updated various times throughout, has played some key roles in studies of this cluster, from finding some of the first multiple images (Zitrin &amp; Broadhurst <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib88\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib88\">2009</a>), through aiding in qualifying it for the HFF via simulations of high-redshift galaxy expectations (Section 3 in Lotz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib47\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib47\">2017</a>), to helping in the investigation of the high-redshift galaxy MACS1149-JD1 (Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib84\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib84\">2012</a>), SN Refsdal (Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib38\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib38\">2015</a>), and the caustic crossing lensed star MACS1149-LS1 (Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib17\">2018</a>; Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib37\">2018</a>). One of our goals here is to present a long-due update of the model. The revised LTM model also contains a fix to a numerical artifact that we uncovered following the blind tests mentioned above (see <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32app1\">Appendix</a>). Our grid-based parametric model for M1149 is constructed on the same grid as the LTM model and using the same general pipeline, but with a different representation of galaxies and dark matter (DM). While preliminary estimates of the TD have been presented in Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>), Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>), and Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>), no such information is used in the minimization for either model. We examine the differences between these two models with respect to the properties of SN Refsdal, and briefly discuss whether more robust TDs and relative magnifications for Refsdal might help distinguish between the models and thus reveal important information about the intrinsic matter distribution (a more profound examination of this is planned for future work). Finally, we also briefly discuss here the implications for the Hubble constant, <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>, of our new TD measurements (e.g., Grillo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib24\">2018</a>; Vega-Ferrero et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib78\">2018</a>) (this is mostly done here in the context of model comparison; we note that a proper determination of the Hubble constant from the lens models requires a more rigorous framework).</p><p>The paper is organized as follows: In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32s2\">2</a> we give a short overview of the modeling methods, and a description of their implementation to M1149 is given in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32s3\">3</a>. The results are presented and discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32s4\">4</a>, with an emphasis on the properties of SN Refsdal, what it may teach us about the underlying matter distribution, and possible implications for the Hubble constant. The work is concluded in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32s5\">5</a>. Throughout this work we use a Lambda cold dark matter cosmology with \u03a9<sub><em>M</em>\n            </sub> = 0.3, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub> = 0.7, and <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> = 70 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>. Unless otherwise stated, errors are 1<em>\u03c3</em>, and we generally use AB magnitudes (Oke &amp; Gunn <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib60\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib60\">1983</a>).</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjac0e32s2\" name=\"apjac0e32s2\">2.\u00a0The Lensing Codes</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjac0e32s2-1\" name=\"apjac0e32s2-1\">2.1.\u00a0The LTM Code</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The LTM code relies on the assumption that light traces mass, so that the weighted luminosity distribution of cluster galaxies can act as a guide for the shape of the total matter distribution. A first version of the code was written and used by Broadhurst et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib9\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib9\">2005</a>) to find dozens of multiple images in A1689, revealing a very large lens. Based on their success, Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib89\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib89\">2009</a>) developed a new, simpler LTM code that included a minimal number of free parameters. This code was since constantly improved, and used to identify multiple images and model dozens of galaxy clusters. Perhaps the greatest value of the code lies in that it is frequently successful in predicting the location of multiple images based only on the luminosity distribution of cluster members, even before it is constrained with any multiple image system (Carrasco et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib11\">2020</a>, see also Zalesky &amp; Ebeling <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib83\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib83\">2020</a>). A second importance is that it is distinct from typical, analytic lens modeling techniques and thus probes a different range of solutions. We give here only a brief overview of the code; for more details see Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib90\">2015</a>, see also the appendix in Carrasco et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib11\">2020</a>).</p><p>The starting point of the model is the distribution of cluster members and their luminosities. Each galaxy is assigned with a power-law mass-density profile, \u03a3(<em>r</em>) \u221d <em>r</em><sup>\u2212<em>q</em>\n               </sup>, scaled in proportion to its luminosity (<em>r</em> is the distance from the center of galaxy). The power-law exponent, <em>q</em>, is a free parameter of the model and the same for all galaxies. A grid is defined\u2014typically matching a portion of the HST image of the cluster\u2014and the total deflection field and mass-density distribution of the galaxies (and all other lensing related quantities) are calculated on that grid. The arbitrarily scaled deflection angle at a certain point <strong>\n                  <em>\u03b8</em>\n               </strong> is simply a linear superposition of all galaxy contributions scaled by their luminosities, or fluxes <em>F</em><sub><em>i</em>\n               </sub> (times some normalization factor):</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn1.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=6MZLEKPggVLZo3cS7g9LkDzp1JY%3D\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{\\mathrm{gal}}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})\\propto \\sum _{i}{F}_{i}\\,| {\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{i}{| }^{1-q}\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{i}}{| {\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{i}| }.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n            </p><p>The corresponding mass-density map of the galaxies, officially in units of the critical density (defined below, see Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjac0e32eqn6\">6</a>)), is then obtained from this deflection field by</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn2.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=PGFhjcJ3Vz3wZtRDg9fCjC%2F%2FxZw%3D\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\kappa }_{\\mathrm{gal}}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})=\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{2}{\\boldsymbol{\\nabla }}{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{\\mathrm{gal}}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}).\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n            </p><p>This <em>\u03ba</em> map is then smoothed (in Fourier space) with a Gaussian kernel to produce a (similarly, arbitrarily scaled) map of the DM component, <em>\u03ba</em><sub>DM</sub>. The width of the Gaussian is a free parameter as well. The deflection field from the galaxies, <strong>\n                  <em>\u03b1</em>\n               </strong><sub>gal</sub>, and that from the DM, <strong>\n                  <em>\u03b1</em>\n               </strong><sub>DM</sub>, the latter calculated from the DM distribution using (Narayan &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib54\">1996</a>)</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn3.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=lHR%2BXDAgIUKQ2ENZk%2FyFb23sSm8%3D\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{\\mathrm{DM}}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})=\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{\\pi }\\int {\\kappa }_{\\mathrm{DM}}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}^{\\prime} )\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}^{\\prime} }{| {\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}^{\\prime} {| }^{2}}{d}^{2}\\theta ^{\\prime} ,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>are then added with a relative weight (determined by <em>K</em><sub>gal</sub>, see Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjac0e32eqn4\">4</a>), which is also a free parameter of the model), and supplemented by a two component external shear whose strength and position angle parameters are typically left free to be optimized as well. Thus, the total deflection field is obtained by combining the three components as follows:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn4.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=Q9nR1cLy0nzojAao6tc7u83IIwg%3D\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{\\mathrm{tot}}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})=K\\,[{K}_{\\mathrm{gal}}{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{\\mathrm{gal}}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})+(1-{K}_{\\mathrm{gal}}){{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{\\mathrm{DM}}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})]+{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{\\mathrm{ex}}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}),\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>K</em> is the overall normalization of the model and the sixth free parameter.</p><p>We often also leave free the core size, relative weight (i.e., the relative mass-to-light ratio), ellipticites (see Equations (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjac0e32eqn8\">8</a>) and (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjac0e32eqn9\">9</a>)), and position angles of key cluster members, such as the BCGs. All other member galaxies are assumed to be circular and core free, for simplicity.</p><p>Once the deflection field is determined, the overall mass-density distribution <em>\u03ba</em>(<strong>\n                  <em>\u03b8</em>\n               </strong>) is then obtained by taking the gradient of the total deflection field, <strong>\n                  <em>\u03b1</em>\n               </strong><sub>tot</sub>(<strong>\n                  <em>\u03b8</em>\n               </strong>), similar to the relation seen in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjac0e32eqn2\">2</a>). The potential is obtained in our procedure by integrating (in Fourier space) this mass-density map, where the relation between the surface mass-density map obtained above and the effective lensing potential is given by (Narayan &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib54\">1996</a>)</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn5\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn5.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=b8qfqRdRXIjyPy2FyONboe%2Bd7g0%3D\" alt=\"Equation (5)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\rm{\\Psi }}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})=\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{\\pi }\\int \\kappa ({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}^{\\prime} )\\mathrm{ln}| {\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}^{\\prime} | {d}^{2}\\theta ^{\\prime} ,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t5\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>\u03ba</em> is the surface mass density in units of the critical density for lensing, i.e., <em>\u03ba</em> = \u03a3/\u03a3<sub>crit</sub>, and</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn6\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn6.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=1ltnNuBKZbI4hu3GeOb3WR7b5%2Fs%3D\" alt=\"Equation (6)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{{\\rm{\\Sigma }}}_{\\mathrm{crit}}=\\displaystyle \\frac{{c}^{2}}{4\\pi G}\\displaystyle \\frac{{D}_{s}}{{D}_{{ls}}{D}_{l}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t6\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>with <em>c</em> the speed of light, and <em>D</em><sub><em>l</em>\n               </sub>, <em>D</em><sub><em>s</em>\n               </sub>, <em>D</em><sub><em>ls</em>\n               </sub> the angular diameter distances to the lens, to the source, and between the lens and the source, respectively.</p><p>To avoid numerical artifacts (see the <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32app1\">Appendix</a>), in each step the potential is calculated as above and constitutes a new starting point for that step in the model, from which all lensing quantities are then self-consistently derived.</p><p>The optimization of the model is carried out by minimizing a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> function that measures the distance of predicted multiple images from their observed location, via a Monte Carlo Markov Chain (MCMC) with a Metropolis\u2013Hastings algorithm. Punishing terms can be added for images with wrong parity or if extra images are predicted. Some annealing is also included in the process (we do a crude, four-step annealing\u2014i.e., the temperature is decreased in four different steps), and the chain typically runs for several thousand steps after the burn-in phase. Errors are then typically calculated from the same, final Markov chain.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjac0e32s2-2\" name=\"apjac0e32s2-2\">2.2.\u00a0The Parametric Code (dPIEeNFW)</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The parametric modeling scheme we use here (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib91\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib91\">2013</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib90\">2015</a>), consists of two main constituents: galaxies and DM halos. Each galaxy is generally modeled as a double pseudo isothermal elliptical mass distribution (dPIE;<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjac0e32fn2\">\n                  2\n               </a></sup>\n                see El\u00edasd\u00f3ttir et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib46\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib46\">2007</a>), and scaled following common scaling relations (e.g., Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib33\">2007</a>; Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib58\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib58\">2015</a>; Monna et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib53\">2017</a>, see below). The projected density for each galaxy is implemented as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn7\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn7.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=CuNgN686qb1FKZ%2Bzj1awmH6UsgQ%3D\" alt=\"Equation (7)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\rm{\\Sigma }}(R)=\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\sigma }_{0}^{2}}{2G}\\displaystyle \\frac{{r}_{\\mathrm{cut}}^{2}}{({r}_{\\mathrm{cut}}^{2}-{r}_{c}^{2})}\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{{r}_{c}^{2}+{R}^{2}}}-\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{{r}_{\\mathrm{cut}}^{2}+{R}^{2}}}\\right),\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t7\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>G</em> the gravitational constant, <em>r</em><sub><em>c</em>\n               </sub> is the galaxy's core radius, <em>r</em><sub>cut</sub> the cutoff radius, and <em>\u03c3</em><sub>0</sub> its velocity dispersion. <em>R</em> is the 2D (elliptical) radius, given as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn8\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn8.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=aXufqWtFphOl5muhFGuok945eAs%3D\" alt=\"Equation (8)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{R}^{2}=\\displaystyle \\frac{{X}^{2}}{{\\left(1+\\epsilon \\right)}^{2}}+\\displaystyle \\frac{{Y}^{2}}{{\\left(1-\\epsilon \\right)}^{2}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t8\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>with <em>X</em> and <em>Y</em> being the spatial coordinates along the major and minor axes, respectively. The relation between these and the original <em>x</em> and <em>y</em> coordinates of the grid, for a galaxy <em>i</em> at (<em>x</em><sub><em>i</em>\n               </sub>, <em>y</em><sub><em>i</em>\n               </sub>) with a position angle <em>\u03d5</em>, is defined by</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn9\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn9.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=j4j%2BqgNZopnZk42r4T5X8V34mWg%3D\" alt=\"Equation (9)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}\\begin{array}{rcl}X & = & (x-{x}_{i})\\,\\cos \\,(\\phi )+(y-{y}_{i})\\,\\sin \\,(\\phi ),\\\\ Y & = & -(x-{x}_{i})\\,\\sin \\,(\\phi )+(y-{y}_{i})\\,\\cos \\,(\\phi ),\\end{array}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t9\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where if <em>x</em> and <em>y</em> are in pixels, an additional factor is needed to translate the grid's pixel coordinates to physical units. The eccentricity, <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/epsi.gif\" alt=\"epsilon\" align=\"baseline\" /></em>, is defined as <em><img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/epsi.gif\" alt=\"epsilon\" align=\"baseline\" /></em> \u2261 (<em>a</em> \u2212 <em>b</em>)/(<em>a</em> + <em>b</em>), where <em>a</em> and <em>b</em> are the semimajor and semiminor axes, respectively (El\u00edasd\u00f3ttir et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib46\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib46\">2007</a>). Another common definition is the ellipticity, defined as <em>e</em> \u2261 1 \u2212 <em>b</em>/<em>a</em>. In practice, only key (i.e., massive, central) galaxies we generally model as elliptical, whereas the rest of the galaxies are modeled as circular for speed-up purposes.</p><p>The relevant quantities for each galaxy are scaled with the galaxy's luminosity <em>L</em>, compared to the values of some reference <em>L</em><sup>\u22c6</sup> galaxy at the cluster's redshift, using the following scaling relation:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn10\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn10.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=B07Mthzwls%2FGNUt6kqkWQTxz20g%3D\" alt=\"Equation (10)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}\\begin{array}{rcl}{\\sigma }_{0} & = & {\\sigma }_{0}^{\\star }{\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{L}{{L}^{\\star }}\\right)}^{\\lambda },\\\\ {r}_{c} & = & {r}_{c}^{\\star }{\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{L}{{L}^{\\star }}\\right)}^{\\beta },\\\\ {r}_{\\mathrm{cut}} & = & {r}_{\\mathrm{cut}}^{\\star }{\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{L}{{L}^{\\star }}\\right)}^{\\alpha },\\end{array}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t10\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where we typically fix <em>\u03bb</em> = 0.25; <em>\u03b2</em> = 0.5; <em>\u03b1</em> = 0.5.</p><p>The second constituent of the model, the DM halos (typically one to a few per cluster), are modeled here as elliptical Navarro\u2013Frenk\u2013White (eNFW; Navarro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib55\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib55\">1996</a>) mass distributions (e.g., Jing &amp; Suto <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib30\">2000</a>; Wright &amp; Brainerd <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib82\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib82\">2000</a>; Meneghetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib50\">2003</a>; Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib58\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib58\">2015</a>). NFW halos follow a radial mass-density profile of the form:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn11\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn11.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=bBE60njtU93PLWGgbMIY%2BHxX6kI%3D\" alt=\"Equation (11)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\rho ({r}_{3})=\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\rho }_{s}}{({r}_{3}/{r}_{s}){\\left(1+{r}_{3}/{r}_{s}\\right)}^{2}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t11\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>with <em>r</em><sub>3</sub> being the three-dimensional radius, <em>r</em><sub><em>s</em>\n               </sub> some scale radius, and <em>\u03c1</em><sub><em>s</em>\n               </sub> the characteristic density of the halo.</p><p>NFW halos can also be described by their concentration and mass. We mark by <em>\u03c1</em><sub>cr</sub> the critical density of the universe at the redshift of the halo. The concentration we employ, <em>c</em><sub>200</sub>, is defined as <em>r</em><sub>200</sub>, the radius inside which the density of the halo equals 200<em>\u03c1</em><sub>cr</sub>, over the scale radius, <em>r</em><sub><em>s</em>\n               </sub>, such that <em>c</em><sub>200</sub> = <em>r</em><sub>200</sub>/<em>r</em><sub><em>s</em>\n               </sub>. The mass, <em>M</em><sub>200</sub>, is the mass enclosed within <em>r</em><sub>200</sub>.</p><p>The projected eNFW surface mass-density distribution is implemented in our code using these concentration and mass definitions, as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn12\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn12.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=ted0mO5LQBFIzH2bG99bbiMo0Yo%3D\" alt=\"Equation (12)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\rm{\\Sigma }}(r)=\\displaystyle \\frac{2{\\rho }_{s}{r}_{s}}{{r}^{2}-1}f(r),\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t12\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>with</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn13\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn13.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=1qMt3UAZwAm4ijBXUbRRs8%2FhrRo%3D\" alt=\"Equation (13)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\rho }_{s}=\\displaystyle \\frac{200}{3}{\\rho }_{\\mathrm{cr}}\\displaystyle \\frac{{c}_{200}^{3}}{[\\mathrm{ln}(1+{c}_{200})-{c}_{200}/(1+{c}_{200})]},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t13\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n               </p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn14\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn14.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=wbDUFIOr8Eg7EPPWhuRbG1AKjsE%3D\" alt=\"Equation (14)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{r}_{s}={\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{\\pi }\\displaystyle \\frac{3}{800}\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{{\\rho }_{\\mathrm{cr}}}\\displaystyle \\frac{{M}_{200}}{{c}_{200}^{3}}\\right)}^{\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{3}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t14\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>and</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn15\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn15.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=PmoxhQbps41tXAy2xg%2FvgpV30TY%3D\" alt=\"Equation (15)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}f(r)=\\left\\{\\begin{array}{cc}1-\\displaystyle \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{{r}^{2}-1}}\\arctan \\sqrt{\\displaystyle \\frac{r-1}{r+1}} & (r\\gt 1)\\\\ 1-\\displaystyle \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{1-{r}^{2}}}\\mathrm{arctanh}\\sqrt{\\displaystyle \\frac{1-r}{1+r}} & (r\\lt 1)\\\\ 0 & (r=1),\\end{array}\\right.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t15\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where the dimensionless <em>r</em> is the 2D (elliptical) radius in units of <em>r</em><sub><em>s</em>\n               </sub>, i.e., <em>r</em> \u2261 <em>R</em>/<em>r</em><sub><em>s</em>\n               </sub>, and the ellipticity and translation to the grid's coordinates are introduced as above (Equations (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjac0e32eqn8\">8</a>) and (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjac0e32eqn9\">9</a>)).</p><p>While analytic expressions for the potential and the deflection fields exist, these are obtained in our procedure by integrating (in Fourier space) the mass-density map, where the relation between the (surface) mass-density map obtained above and the effective lensing potential is given by (Narayan &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib54\">1996</a>)</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn16\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn16.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=7%2FShp6rAwa30saaOfuu%2FAX3XROo%3D\" alt=\"Equation (16)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\rm{\\Psi }}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})=\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{\\pi }\\int \\kappa ({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}^{\\prime} )\\mathrm{ln}| {\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}^{\\prime} | {d}^{2}\\theta ^{\\prime} ,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t16\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>and the relation between the surface mass-density map and the deflection field is given by</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn17\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn17.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=VbLbF5Eju0Cxv7T6lnH76G52xkA%3D\" alt=\"Equation (17)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})={\\rm{\\nabla }}{\\rm{\\Psi }}=\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{\\pi }\\int \\kappa ({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}^{\\prime} )\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}^{\\prime} }{| {\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}^{\\prime} {| }^{2}}{d}^{2}\\theta ^{\\prime} .\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t17\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n            </p><p>Also here, to avoid numerical artifacts described in the <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32app1\">Appendix</a>, in each step the potential is calculated as above and constitutes a new starting point for the model from which all lensing quantities are then self-consistently derived.</p><p>In addition to the two main components, i.e., the galaxies and DM halos, an external shear can be in principle added directly to the deflection field.</p><p>In cases where this is needed to refine the fit and assuming sufficient constraints are available, we often leave as free parameters the core size, relative weight (i.e., the mass or relative mass-to-light ratio), ellipticites, and position angles of key cluster members, such as the BCGs. The optimization of the model is performed with the same pipeline as for our LTM model (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32s2-1\">2.1</a>), using a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> function that minimizes the distance between the predicted multiple images (using a simply averaged source position for each system) and their observed locations and via MCMC.</p><p>We refer to the model, in short, after the combination of underlying forms adopted for the galaxies and DM, i.e., dPIEeNFW.</p></div></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjac0e32s3\" name=\"apjac0e32s3\">3.\u00a0Lens Modeling of M1149</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjac0e32s3-1\" name=\"apjac0e32s3-1\">3.1.\u00a0LTM Modeling of M1149</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>As mentioned, the first SL LTM model for M1149 was constructed in 2009 with an old code, prior to CLASH multiband data, and before redshifts were available for this cluster. This model was later updated with improvements to the code and using CLASH data or newer products agreed on within the HFF framework, and included SL, or strong+weak lensing constraints combined (e.g., Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib90\">2015</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>; Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib85\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib85\">2017</a>). Here, we update the model submitted to the Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>), Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>), and Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>) blind tests with SN Refsdal, with a corrected TD calculation (see the <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32app1\">Appendix</a>).</p><p>Since the model is limited to the grid resolution and our goal here is to examine the properties of SN Refsdal, we concentrate on the central \u223c140'' <b>&#x00d7;</b> 140'' area of the cluster. The final model has the same spatial resolution as the model presented in Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>), of 0.065'' pix<sup>\u22121</sup>, native to the public CLASH HST images (although some parts in the minimization are done in a few times lower resolution for speed-up purposes).</p><p>We leave the weight of four galaxies free: the central BCG (R.A. = 11:49:35.70, decl. = +22:23:54.71); a second bright cluster member at R.A. = 11:49:37.55, decl. = +22:23:22.49; another bright member next to the center at R.A. = 11:49:36.86, decl. = +22:23:46.97; and the galaxy that lenses SN Refsdal into the Einstein cross (R.A. = 11:49:35.47, decl. = +22:23:43.63). The ellipticity and position angle of the main BCG are left free to be optimized around their values measured by SExtractor (Bertin &amp; Arnouts <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib5\">1996</a>), and the ellipticity and position angle of the second and third BCGs are kept fixed on their measured values. A core is assumed for the central BCG, whose size is a free parameter. The general galaxy power-law parameter, <em>q</em>, is fixed to 1.45, based on various trials that have shown that this value works very well in reproducing the partial spiral counter image north of the BCG (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f3\">3</a>).</p><p>The multiple image constraints we use here are given in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t2\">2</a>. These consist mainly of the <em>gold</em> multiply imaged systems presented in Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>) and Finney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib20\">2018</a>), with some <em>silver</em> counter images that our model predicts as secure. The <em>gold/silver/etc.</em> ranking was given based on voting by lens modeling teams in the framework HFF and SN Refsdal's efforts (Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>, for details). We also adopt the detailed mapping of the main, lensed spiral galaxy's internal knots as presented in Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>, and references therein) and Finney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib20\">2018</a>), and use these as additional constraints, as listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t3\">3</a>. All systems were fixed to their spectroscopic redshift where available. Systems 6 and 7 do not have a spectroscopic measurement and their redshift was fixed to \u22432.6 based on their photo-<em>z</em>. We do not use any measured TDs or magnification ratios as input.</p><p>For the <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> calculation, a positional uncertainty of 0.5'' is adopted for most multiple images, but for the four SN images in the Einstein cross we adopt 0.1''. We also include punishing terms to guarantee the correct parity of these images, and to verify that no additional (complete) image of the spiral galaxy is predicted just north of the BCG, which was the case in some trial models.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjac0e32s3-2\" name=\"apjac0e32s3-2\">3.2.\u00a0dPIEeNFW Modeling of M1149</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>For constraining the parametric model for M1149 we use the same list of constraints as for the LTM model (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32s3-1\">3.1</a>), namely, the gold sample from Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>) and Finney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib20\">2018</a>), with some minor additions of silver images listed in Tables <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t2\">2</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t3\">3</a>. Similar to the LTM model, we employ for the <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> function a positional uncertainty of <em>\u03c3</em><sub>pos</sub> = 0.5'' for most images, and for the four Einstein cross SN images we adopt <em>\u03c3</em><sub>pos</sub> = 0.1'', and we fix the redshift of systems 6 and 7 to \u22432.6 based on their photo-<em>z</em>. Also here, we do not use any measured TDs or magnification ratios as input.</p><p>We include the same list of cluster galaxies as in our LTM model\u2014namely, a red-sequence based selection, cross matched with available spectroscopic information (Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>, and references therein). We fix the ellipticity of the three brightest galaxies (R.A. = 11:49:35.70, decl. = +22:23:54.71; R.A. = 11:49:36.9328, decl. = +22:25:35.882; R.A. = 11:49:37.55, decl. = +22:23:22.49) to their measured values and refer to all other galaxies as circular. We employ a vanishing core for all galaxies, i.e., fixing <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32ieqn2.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=tSeClgWyqdBNz0Kd6%2B39Xcr5QZY%3D\" alt=\"${r}_{\\mathrm{core}}^{\\star }=0$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{r}_{\\mathrm{core}}^{\\star }=0</script></span></span>, but allow for a finite core for the BCG, leaving the core size free to be optimized in the minimization.</p><p>Following previous work (e.g., Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib58\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib58\">2015</a>; Sharon &amp; Johnson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib72\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib72\">2015</a>; Kawamata et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib35\">2016</a>; Grillo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib24\">2018</a>), we include in the modeling three DM halos: one is centered on the BCG (R.A. = 11:49:35.70, decl. = +22:23:54.71), with the exact position to be optimized in the modeling; one is centered on a bright galaxy about 40'' southeast of the BCG (R.A. = 11:49:37.55, decl. = +22:23:22.49), and one is around a clump of galaxies about 50'' roughly north of the BCG (at R.A. = 11:49:34.52, decl. = +22:24:42.09), whose exact position is also free to be determined in the minimization. We do not include an external shear in the current model.</p><p>We build our model on the same grid as the LTM model, concentrated on the central \u223c140'' <b>&#x00d7;</b> 140'' area of the cluster, and with a resolution of 0.065'' pix<sup>\u22121</sup> (some parts in the minimization are done in a few times lower resolution for speed-up purposes, but the final chain is run with the native resolution).</p></div></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjac0e32s4\" name=\"apjac0e32s4\">4.\u00a0Results</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjac0e32s4-1\" name=\"apjac0e32s4-1\">4.1.\u00a0LTM Results</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Our resulting LTM mass model is seen in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f1\">1</a>, along with the magnification map and TD surface contours. The final model has an image reproduction rms of 0.68'', and a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> \u2243 242. With the many multiple images used, the number of constraints is <em>N</em><sub><em>c</em>\n               </sub> = 170, and the number of free parameters <em>N</em><sub><em>p</em>\n               </sub> = 11, such that the number of degrees of freedom is DOF = <em>N</em><sub><em>c</em>\n               </sub> \u2212 <em>N</em><sub><em>p</em>\n               </sub> = 159. Hence, the <em>reduced \u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> is \u22431.5. We note that for the spiral knots alone the rms is a bit lower, 0.49'' (note also the rms is limited by the grid's resolution; a quantification of this effect will be done elsewhere).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjac0e32f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjac0e32f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=MiCCCvbAoSoCOgIAQjtsEe6AUJU%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=MiCCCvbAoSoCOgIAQjtsEe6AUJU%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Our revised LTM SL model for M1149. Upper left panel shows the surface mass density, <em>\u03ba</em>, for the redshift of system 1, the spiral galaxy at <em>z</em> = 1.49, with the positions of S1\u2013S4, SX, and SY, marked with white circles; the upper right panel shows the magnification map for that redshift, similarly marking the SN image positions; the bottom left panel shows contours of the TD surface with respect to the SN image S1, in decrements of 0.5 yr, marking with red circles the Einstein cross images and SX; the bottom right panel shows contours of the TD surface with respect to the SN image S1, in decrements of 10 days, marking with red circles the positions of the Einstein cross images.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjac0e32f1\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=MiCCCvbAoSoCOgIAQjtsEe6AUJU%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjac0e32f1\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f1_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=LcUBzHCkzBHjmr1uwhNZ4M7UdCg%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The reproduction of the complex configuration of system 1 can also constitute a (more qualitative, in our case) testimony for the model's credibility in the central region. We show the reproduction of this system in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f3\">3</a>, compared to the data, by sending the largest spiral image to the source plane and back. The reproduction of some knots is not very prominent, perhaps indicative of some inaccuracy or a resolution limit, although overall the model seems to predict very well the detailed appearance of the images.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjac0e32s4-2\" name=\"apjac0e32s4-2\">4.2.\u00a0LTM TDs and Magnifications for SN Refsdal</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The model's TDs and magnification ratios, with respect to S1, for SN Refsdal's five observed images (S1\u2013S4 and SX), as well as the older unobserved SN image (SY), are given in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t1\">1</a>. The quoted errors were derived using a 100 random models from a designated MC chain, and we list both the 68.3% and 95% confidence intervals. To account for (some) systematics, these were extracted using an effective <em>\u03c3</em><sub>pos</sub> \u2243 1.7'', which we have found better captures the range of values from different trial models constructed in the course of modeling M1149 with the LTM method. The original statistical intervals are about 3\u20135 times smaller. The relevant values seem to broadly agree, in most cases, with the early measurements by Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>) and Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>), listed in the same table as well. However, we anticipate that updated measurements for SN Refsdal, exploiting a wider monitoring time span of Refsdal's appearances, will become available in the near future. While a major discrepancy between such updated measurements and our current estimate may persist, we note that our numbers seem to very broadly agree with the range of estimates from the different models seen in Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>), Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>), and Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>). Most models predict TDs of order days for S2 and S3 with respect to S1; about 15\u201330 days between S4 and S1, and from \u223c220 to \u223c380 days between SX and S1. Nevertheless, it seems that the LTM TD estimates, and most notably for the SX-S1 TD, are systematically lower than those obtained by most parametric techniques. In addition, if updated SX-S1 TD measurements remain significantly different than our estimate, it can potentially cast important clues on properties of the underlying matter distribution that are critical for TD mapping. Based on the early measurements by Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>) the TD is not very likely to be shorter than our estimate (see also Baklanov et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib4\">2021</a>), and is more likely to be significantly larger: following Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>) SX was detected on 2015 December 11, and shows fainter traces also on 2015 November 14\u2014almost exactly a year after detecting the Einstein cross on 2014 November 10. In contrast, observations from 2015 October 30 yielded no statistically significant detection of SX. Hence, as seen in Figure 3 in Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>), unless the magnification of SX is very small compared to S1, most chances are that the SX-S1 TD is larger than our LTM estimate of \u223c224 days (or \u223c267 days, depending on the source position, see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32s4-6\">4.6</a>).</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjac0e32t1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0TDs and Magnification Ratios
