align=\"center\">0.24 [0.22\u20130.24] [0.21\u20130.25]</td></tr></tbody></table><p><small><p>\n                     <strong>Notes.</strong> Early measurements of SN Refsdal's TDs (in days) and magnification ratios, along with estimates from our new LTM and dPIEeNFW models. Note that TDs and magnification ratios were not used as constraints in the minimization, and similarly, any information regarding SX (and SY) was not used explicitly as well. We anticipate updated and more accurate measurements for SN Refsdal will become available in the near future.</p><a name=\"apjac0e32t1fna\" id=\"apjac0e32t1fna\"></a><sup> \n                        a\n                     </sup>Taken from Table 3 in Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>) based on a set of templates (<em>t</em>), or on polynomials (<em>p</em>) (this notation is adopted from Grillo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib24\">2018</a>).<a name=\"apjac0e32t1fnb\" id=\"apjac0e32t1fnb\"></a><sup> \n                        b\n                     </sup>Taken from Grillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib24\">2018</a>), as estimated from Figure 3 in Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>).<a name=\"apjac0e32t1fnc\" id=\"apjac0e32t1fnc\"></a><sup> \n                        c\n                     </sup>If instead the source position is derived, in addition to the Einstein cross images, using also the position of SX, the LTM TD about 40\u201350 days longer and likely closer to the true value.</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/919/1/54/suppdata/apjac0e32t1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/ac0e32\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32t1_lr.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=0BkUUHRCpIDhte5mfXGGkX7fJbw%3D\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>The absolute magnifications are not listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t1\">1</a>, and instead given here. Our model predicts magnifications of <em>\u03bc</em> \u223c 20\u201322 for the images S1\u2013S3, <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0\u2243\u00a05.2 for S4, and <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0\u2243\u00a04.7 for SX. SX is predicted at R.A. = 177.39995, decl. = 22.39664 by our model, about \u22430.4'' from its observed location in Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>). The model suggests that SY appeared \u224318 yr ago, with a magnification of <em>\u03bc</em> \u223c 3.4.</p><p>It is worth mentioning that in the presented model, the effective ellipticity of the galaxy that forms the Einstein cross was not optimized separately; the galaxy was modeled as a simple circular power-law, and the ellipticity of its critical curve, which produces the four images of the SN, was automatically obtained as such likely due to the shear caused by the main potential well, centered on the BCG. It is conceivable that an independent modeling of this lensing galaxy could yield more refined results. Similarly, we remind the reader that the LTM code is limited to the grid's resolution, which could affect the TD measurement. To assess the typical magnitude of the effect, we repeat the TD measurements from our model with a resolution twice lower in each axis. The TDs between the images of the Einstein cross change by about few to \u223c10 days (and so the estimate for the order of images S1\u2013S3 can also change), and the TD between SX and S1, as well as the confidence interval, increase by about a month. As for the magnification ratios, while most typically agree within 1\u20132<em>\u03c3</em> with the higher-resolution result, some may be more severely affected\u2014especially those images near the critical curve (i.e., the Einstein cross images) and the higher-resolution results are needed to obtain more stable predictions for them. In cases where very high-resolution results are needed, analytic codes (which essentially can obtain any desired resolution) may be preferable, although in some cases higher-resolution grids could also be employed with LTM.</p><p>Finally, we briefly mention that, by design, the range of different trial models we constructed in the process of remodeling M1149 (specifically models with rms &lt; 1''), spanning a range of input configurations (number of freely weighted galaxies and which, ellipticity of the BCGs, number of systems with free redshifts, etc.), typically fall within the range of errors for our best-fit model presented in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t1\">1</a>. The majority of these models give values for the SX-S1 TD of \u223c170 days to \u223c340 days, most concentrated around 200\u2013260 days, but few\u2014usually the less well-constrained models\u2014give larger or smaller TDs.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjac0e32s4-3\" name=\"apjac0e32s4-3\">4.3.\u00a0dPIEeNFW Results</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The surface mass-density and magnification maps form our dPIEeNFW model, as well as TD surface contours are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f2\">2</a>. The final model has an image reproduction rms of 0.57'', and a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> \u2243 170. The number of constraints following the multiple image list is <em>N</em><sub><em>c</em>\n               </sub> = 170, and the number of free parameters is <em>N</em><sub><em>p</em>\n               </sub> = 21. Correspondingly, the number of degrees of freedom is DOF = <em>N</em><sub><em>c</em>\n               </sub> \u2212 <em>N</em><sub><em>p</em>\n               </sub> = 149. The <em>reduced \u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> is thus \u22431.14. For comparison, recall the LTM has an rms of 0.68'', a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> \u2243 242, and a reduced <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> \u2243 1.5, such that both are of roughly similar accuracy (the rms is \u223c15% lower for the parametric model) but the parametric method does seem to yield a better fit overall and better match to the data.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjac0e32f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjac0e32f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f2_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=46NT4LoKUicvUV15LSzEpRJMxK8%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f2_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=46NT4LoKUicvUV15LSzEpRJMxK8%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Our parametric dPIEeNFW model for M1149. Upper left figure shows <em>\u03ba</em>, the surface mass density in units of the critical density for lensing, scaled to the redshift of system 1, the spiral galaxy hosting SN Refsdal, at <em>z</em> = 1.49. The positions of S1\u2013S4, SX, and SY are marked with white circles. Upper right panel shows the magnification map from the model for that redshift, similarly marking the SN image positions. Bottom left panel shows contours of the TD surface with respect to the SN image S1, in decrements of 0.5 yr (some contours are not labeled for better view), marking with red circles the Einstein cross images and and SX. Bottom right panel shows contours of the TD surface with respect to the SN image S1, in decrements of 10 days, marking with red circles the positions of the Einstein cross images.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjac0e32f2\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f2_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=46NT4LoKUicvUV15LSzEpRJMxK8%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjac0e32f2\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f2_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=w3dmcSLWcy1FnYkEwcqxfpmOkAw%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We also show here (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f3\">3</a>) the reproduction of system 1, the spiral galaxy, by the parametric model\u2014compared to the data and to the LTM model. Although some local inaccuracies exist, the detailed reproduction is evidently quite successful (although somewhat distinct) in both models.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjac0e32f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjac0e32f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f3_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=D3NC0wLFJQb9B%2FuEkEHeinIAhqo%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f3_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=D3NC0wLFJQb9B%2FuEkEHeinIAhqo%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Reproduction of system 1, the spiral galaxy hosting SN Refsdal. Upper row shows the images in the original data. We send the largest image, 1.1, to the source plane and back, to obtain the reproduction of the other images of this system by our LTM (middle row) and parametric (bottom row) models. The first stamp is 7.8'' on 7.8'' and the three other stamps are 6.5'' on 6.5''.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjac0e32f3\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f3_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=D3NC0wLFJQb9B%2FuEkEHeinIAhqo%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjac0e32f3\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f3_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=Bcs0mapAobO9JmEtKXK9MLvEHd0%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjac0e32s4-4\" name=\"apjac0e32s4-4\">4.4.\u00a0dPIEeNFW TDs and Magnifications for SN Refsdal</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The dPIEeNFW model TDs with respect to S1 are given in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t1\">1</a>. The quoted errors were derived using a 100 random models from a designated MC chain, and we list therein both the 68.3% and 95% confidence intervals. These were obtained by adopting an effective <em>\u03c3</em><sub>pos</sub> \u2243 0.7'', which better encompasses the range of values from different dPIEeNFW models we generated during the final modeling of M1149.</p><p>Our model predicts that among the Einstein cross images, S1 arrived first; S3 arrived second, about 3 days later; S2 arrived about 10 days after S1; and S4 about \u224324 days after S1 (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t1\">1</a> for exact numbers). The SX-S1 TD and 95% CI is predicted to be \u223c326 [300\u2013359] days. According to our model, SY appeared close to 18 yr ago. The best-fit magnifications we obtain for the different images are [17.3, 20.2, 19.6, 11.4, 4.4, 4.1] for [S1, S2, S3, S4, SX, SY], respectively. The magnification ratios and their uncertainties are listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t1\">1</a> as well.</p><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f5\">5</a> we plot both our LTM and dPIEeNFW estimates for SN Refsdal, along with the early measurements by Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>) and Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>). We also show therein, for comparison, predictions from other parametric models that appeared in Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>). The values from our parametric, dPIEeNFW model seem to be in a very good agreement (typically to within 1<em>\u03c3</em> or 2<em>\u03c3</em>) with the predictions from the other parametric models applied to M1149, especially with those from the <em>Grillo</em> and <em>Oguri</em> models (see Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a> and Table 6 in Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>). They also agree well with the early SN measurements shown therein and listed here in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t1\">1</a> (although in some cases there is some disagreement\u2014for example for S4). In addition, during the writing of this work another measurement was published by Baklanov et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib4\">2021</a>) by using the Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>) photometry and hydrodynamics modeling of the explosion, which we also include in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f5\">5</a>. These new measurements seem to be in very good agreement with the numbers from our parametric model as well.</p><p>Despite the good agreement with both the predictions from other models (see also Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib58\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib58\">2015</a>; Grillo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib24\">2018</a>), and with the measurements of the observed SN images, it should be noted that the modeling scheme was not tailored to model the Einstein cross in high detail: First, we work on a grid, and so our solution is limited to the grid's finite resolution, which is non-negligible compared to the distances of the cross' images from the lensing galaxy's critical curves, for example. This also likely causes the rms to be somewhat higher than purely analytic methods (although the rms, especially between different methods, is not a very credible estimator for a model's accuracy or prediction power; see also Johnson &amp; Sharon <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib31\">2016</a>). Second, while we leave free the weight (or mass) of the galaxy around which the Einstein cross forms, we include no ellipticity for it, nor do we separately optimize its core and cutoff radii (these instead were assumed to follow the scaling relation, where the core radius was set to zero here). We speculate that <em>analytic</em> methods, i.e., those not confined to a certain grid resolution, could in principle obtain higher-resolution results where such accuracy is needed. In the case of SN Refsdal, however, it seems that despite these limitations our grid-based model is comparable to that from other parametric methods.</p><p>Our dPIEeNFW model is also publicly available.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjac0e32fn3\">\n                  3\n               </a></sup>\n               \n            </p></div><h3 id=\"apjac0e32s4-5\" name=\"apjac0e32s4-5\">4.5.\u00a0Comparison of the Two Models</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>While our parametric model agrees very well with the typical predictions from other parametric lens models that were applied to SN Refsdal (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f5\">5</a>; see also Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>), the LTM model seems to yield somewhat smaller magnification ratios for all images, with respect to S1, and systematically shorter TDs for three out of the five examined TDs: SX-S1, S2-S1, and S3-S1. While most (although not all) parametric methods, including our own presented here, yield a TD of order \u223c300\u2013360 days for the appearance of SX after S1, the LTM model suggests a systematically lower TD 95% CI of \u223c200\u2013300 days (or \u223c250\u2013320 days, if adopting a revised source position using information on SX; see also Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjac0e32s4-6\">4.6</a>). For the Einstein cross images S2 and S3, the LTM model suggests 6 days instead of 10 for the S2-S1 TD, and 1\u20132 days instead of 3\u20134 days for the S3-S1 TD. The S4-S1 TD seems to be similar in the two models.</p><p>To highlight the differences between the models, in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f4\">4</a> we show the ratio of the dPIEeNFW model to the LTM model, in terms of mass density, magnification, and TD. Recall both models were constructed using the same pipeline, with the same grid resolution, same list of cluster members as input and same multiple image constraints. The main difference between the two methods lies in the parameterization. While it is unclear at present which exact properties of the parameterizations play a significant role here (this naturally deserves a separate and more quantitative examination, to be done elsewhere), we can speculate on the potential causes. In particular, we list three principal differences between the two parameterizations, and discuss to what extent they may affect the solution:</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">(i)\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Cluster galaxies are generally modeled as dPIE in the parametric model, i.e., they have a pseudo isothermal mass-density profile and a cutoff radius. In contrast, galaxies in the LTM methodology are modeled each as a power-law mass distribution, with no cutoff, and a profile steeper than isothermal (also, the power-law exponent is typically a free parameter because it affects the overall profile of the cluster). In both methodologies galaxies are modeled as circular with only a few key ones having ellipticity assigned to them.On the one hand, given most of the lensing occurs on cluster scales, and since the deflection angle from each mass component only depends on the interior mass within some radius of interest, the exact small-scale distribution should not have a major effect on the global model properties. On the other hand, since the Einstein cross images are primarily lensed by a cluster <em>galaxy</em>, smaller-scale perturbation by this galaxy could be important, so the different parameterization may affect the solutions for the cross. The two TD surfaces concentrated on this galaxy, shown in the bottom right panels of Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f1\">1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f2\">2</a>, seem overall similar, indicating that the exact parameterization of this galaxy does not play a crucial role in, e.g., the SX-S1 TD. It could account, however, for the more minor (but statistically significant) differences between the Einstein cross images. The bottom right panel in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f4\">4</a> shows that there is a local, significant difference caused around the lensing galaxy, likely a result of its different parameterization in the two models. The difference at the image positions reaches a factor of \u223c1.5\u20132 in TDs with respect to S1, which for order \u223c10 days can reach \u223c15\u201320 days. Note also, the uncertainties on the magnification ratios from our parametric model are often significantly smaller than those from other parametric lens models. This could be a result of other models independently modeling the Einstein cross lens galaxy, whereas in our case, besides its mass, we left its properties coupled to the scaling relations. Additionally, the small positional error we assigned to the SN images at the Einstein cross may also play a role in lowering the uncertainties, compared to other models.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">(ii)\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">The DM distribution in the parametric case is modeled nearly independently of the galaxy component, or the galaxy light distribution, whereas in the LTM methodology the DM map is coupled to the galaxy component (the DM map is a smoothed version of the galaxies' luminosity-weighted distribution). In that sense the parametric method has more freedom and flexibility to describe the multiple images more closely, and often (including here) results in an overall better fit to the data, and thus, in principle, higher accuracy. On the other hand, the LTM can probe a different range of DM profile shapes as it is not coupled to a certain analytical form (or combinations thereof), and it has unprecedented prediction power to predict the appearance of multiple images based solely on the luminosity distribution as input (e.g., Carrasco et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib11\">2020</a>; Zalesky &amp; Ebeling <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib83\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib83\">2020</a>). It is tempting to claim that the somewhat higher accuracy of the dPIEeNFW model (rms = 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />57) compared to the LTM model (rms = 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />68) suggests that the parametric methodology leads to better estimates for SN Refsdal, and SX-S1 in particular. However, the LTM model has half the number of free parameters, and it is conceivable that future versions, allowing for more free parameters (more free galaxy masses, for example), will help improve the fit further. In addition, given that the predictions from the range of probed LTM models constructed in the process of this work, were systematically lower than the range from the parametric models constructed, the higher accuracy of the parametric model does not necessarily seem to be the cause.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">(iii)\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Another difference that follows the different DM representation in the two methods is the role, or implementation, of ellipticity. The parametric model we employ here includes elliptical NFW halos for the DM, so that the ellipticity of each halo is embedded in the mass distribution itself, whereas in the LTM model there is no global ellipticity embedded in the mass distribution (only that introduced for individual galaxies). The parametric model also does not require an external shear to obtain a good fit, whereas the external shear in the LTM case was found to be quite strong (about <em>\u03b3</em><sub>ex</sub> \u2243 0.2 along the main elongation direction of the cluster), imitating ellipticity in the critical curves, but the mass distribution in the center itself is rounder (Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f1\">1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f2\">2</a>), especially the central part. In addition, unlike the LTM model, the parametric modeling has been shown to require a third, bright or massive clump north of the BCG (\u223c1.2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>14</sup> \n                        <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> in our model; see also Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f4\">4</a>). This bright clump may partly replace the need for an external shear, although it produces different effects in the mass distribution (and critical curves). The lack of constraints in the northern part of the lens around that third clump does not allow for a detailed determination of its true weight. Alternatively, the lack of images may suggest, unless contributed by local, cosmic variance, that perhaps that third clump is not as massive as indicated by most parametric methods, and thus may hint that the LTM parameterization is more correct around that clump, in that sense. Future data (as well as other measurements such as weak lensing, possibly) will be needed to examine the weight of that clump and the possible degeneracies between this clump, the inherent matter ellipticity, and an external shear.</div></li></ul><p>\n            </p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjac0e32f4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjac0e32f4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f4_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=CO4Wtd4RlUfqme%2Fbj7GOau1MDQM%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f4_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=CO4Wtd4RlUfqme%2Fbj7GOau1MDQM%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Comparison of the two models. The subfigures show the ratio of the dPIEeNFW and LTM models in <em>\u03ba</em>, the mass-density map (upper left); the magnification map (upper right); the TD surface with respect to S1 (bottom left); and the TD surface with respect to S1, zoomed in on the Einstein cross (bottom right). <em>White circles</em> mark the SN image positions. See the text for discussion.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjac0e32f4\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f4_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=CO4Wtd4RlUfqme%2Fbj7GOau1MDQM%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjac0e32f4\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f4_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=o7CnKguiNa4i7zqHSya4TQwniF8%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>More detailed examinations of these features are warranted, and remain for future work.</p><p>It may be somewhat surprising that while the two mass maps in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f1\">1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f2\">2</a> are notably, quite distinct (upper left panels; especially, the LTM model seems to have more mass associated with bright galaxies also away for the center, compared to the more concentrated dPIEeNFW model; see also Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f4\">4</a>), the overall reproduction rms is not very different (15% better for the parametric method). In addition, the overall TD surface (third row) seems to be relatively similar in shape, but with some subtle differences near the position of SX, leading to shorter delays in the flatter, LTM case.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjac0e32s4-6\" name=\"apjac0e32s4-6\">4.6.\u00a0The Importance of Chosen Source Position</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The effect of the adopted source position on the TDs should also be discussed (e.g., Birrer &amp; Treu <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib6\">2019</a>). For our LTM model, the TDs are significantly affected by the exact position of the source, which can be obtained in various ways. We considered two source positions to derive the TDs, and the SX-S1 TD in particular. One source position was obtained by delensing only the four images of the Einstein cross and taking their average source position. The second source position included also the delensed position of SX in the average, even though SX was not used in the minimization. This second source position yielded SX-S1 LTM TDs that are about 40\u201350 days larger than with the first source position (this higher TD likely more correct; although here we adopt the former for a fair comparison), and it changed the TDs of the Einstein cross images by order days. The TDs quoted by the first source position are probably more representative of the properties of S1\u2013S4, whereas it remains to be seen if the TD taken from the second source position including SX, describes better the SX-S1 TD, as one may speculate. In that sense the <em>first</em> SX-S1 TD [68.3%][95%], 224.4 [221.4\u2013272.7] [197.8\u2013305.5] days, is the one that would have been given prior to the detection of SX, had we used the current model\u2014and it seems that it matches almost exactly the old LTM TD estimate in Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>), but with a lower SX/S1 magnification ratio.</p><p>For our dPIEeNFW model we find, perhaps due to the higher accuracy, that the SX-S1 TD increases by about 15 days only, if considering the second source position, compared to the nominal values listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t1\">1</a> using the first source position. The effect on the Einstein cross is smaller, of the order of days, similar to what was seen in the LTM case.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjac0e32s4-7\" name=\"apjac0e32s4-7\">4.7.\u00a0Implications for the Hubble Constant</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The equation that describes the delayed arrival time of each image, due to the presence of the lens, compared to an undeflected light ray from the same source, is given by (Narayan &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib54\">1996</a>)</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn18\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn18.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=sxO4hLtaKDWd0%2Ff%2BaaEbqK%2BXdZk%3D\" alt=\"Equation (18)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&t({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})=\\displaystyle \\frac{1+{z}_{l}}{c}\\displaystyle \\frac{{D}_{l}{D}_{s}}{{D}_{{ls}}}\\left[\\displaystyle \\frac{1}{2}{\\left({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{\\boldsymbol{\\beta }}\\right)}^{2}-\\psi ({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})\\right],\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t18\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <strong>\n                  <em>\u03b8</em>\n               </strong> is the image position, <strong>\n                  <em>\u03b2</em>\n               </strong> is the source position as indicated by the lens model, and <em>\u03c8</em> the lensing potential given by the lens model). <em>D</em><sub><em>l</em>\n               </sub>, <em>D</em><sub><em>s</em>\n               </sub>, and <em>D</em><sub><em>ls</em>\n               </sub> are the angular diameter distances to the lens, to the source, and between the lens and the source, respectively. We denote the arrival time difference between the different multiple images simply as the TD.</p><p>The TD distance, i.e., the combination of angular diameter distances, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32ieqn3.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=QQ5x2PshwRU9JRY7nNC2aH1fDJc%3D\" alt=\"$\\tfrac{{D}_{l}{D}_{s}}{{D}_{{ls}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\tfrac{{D}_{l}{D}_{s}}{{D}_{{ls}}}</script></span></span> (times (1 + <em>z</em><sub><em>l</em>\n               </sub>)), is inversely proportional to the Hubble constant. Thus, TD measurements can be used to constrain the Hubble constant. It should be stressed that a proper determination of the Hubble constant and its posterior distribution given the data requires a rigorous statistical framework, possibly taking into account the TDs already in the minimization of the model (e.g., Grillo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib24\">2018</a>). However, to a reasonable approximation sufficient for our purpose here, one can obtain a rough estimate by simply rescaling the model's TD, using the measured TD:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjac0e32eqn19\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32eqn19.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=14ZcZHB0qNipGWsK8Y9%2F8SS1WvM%3D\" alt=\"Equation (19)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{H}_{0,\\mathrm{true}}={\\mathrm{TD}}_{\\mathrm{model}}/{\\mathrm{TD}}_{\\mathrm{true}}\\cdot {H}_{0,\\mathrm{model}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t19\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where we used in our modeling <em>H</em><sub>0,model</sub> = 70 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>. The final, measured TD for Refsdal is yet unknown at the time of this writing. If, for example, we use the TD<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32ieqn4.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=4T%2FZpNqh%2F5VFa6Eq9KNX23%2F2Tp0%3D\" alt=\"${}_{\\mathrm{true}}={340}_{-52}^{+43}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{}_{\\mathrm{true}}={340}_{-52}^{+43}</script></span></span> between S1 and SX recently measured by Baklanov et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib4\">2021</a>) (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f5\">5</a> here), the Hubble constant implied by our parametric model would be \u224367 \u00b1 10 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>. The measurement in Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>) is similar, concentrated around \u223c345 days (with about a 10% error). For comparison, as our LTM model suggests systematically smaller SX-S1 TDs, by about 20%\u201330%, it correspondingly leads to a much smaller Hubble constant, for the same measurement by Baklanov et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib4\">2021</a>) or Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>), although the LTM prediction likely underestimates the true reappearance time: The Hubble constant implied by the LTM model would be as low as \u223c46 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, with a 95% CI of \u2243[40\u201362] km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, in the case where the cross' source position is adopted; or \u223c54 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup> with a 95% CI of \u2243[51\u201365] km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, in the case where the mean cross+SX source position is adopted.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjac0e32f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjac0e32f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=3IrLS8HYqZ657Niu8PvHV2KTwpo%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=3IrLS8HYqZ657Niu8PvHV2KTwpo%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0Comparison of TDs and magnification ratios from our new LTM and parametric dPIEeNFW models with early measurements of SN Refsdal and other lens models. All measurements are with respect to S1. Our dPIEeNFW model is plotted in blue and marked for emphasis with a larger circle. Our LTM model is plotted with a dashed\u2013dotted darker green line and marked with a larger diamond for emphasis. We also overplot with a black dotted line embedded in a gray rectangle the early measurements for SN Refsdal, where available (SY does not have a measurement). These are read of Figure 3 in Kelly et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>) for SX (showing here only approximate uncertainties and not the full confidence region shape shown therein), and from Rodney et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>) for S2, S3, and S4 using their template-fit results. We also show in a black dashed line embedded in a gray rectangle, the recent measurements from Baklanov et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib4\">2021</a>). Other parametric lens models submitted to the blind test by Treu et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>) are also over-plotted. Most values were taken from Table 6 therein, whereas the Jauzac entry was read off Figure 3 in Kelly et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjac0e32f5\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=3IrLS8HYqZ657Niu8PvHV2KTwpo%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjac0e32f5\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f5_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=PaoCKS1%2FELs55cqn6%2Bcctxxf9sQ%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>An another comparison, note that previous measurements of the Hubble constant with SN Refsdal took place before (e.g., see Vega-Ferrero et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib78\">2018</a> and Grillo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib24\">2018</a>; Baklanov et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib4\">2021</a>), that are generally, and unsurprisingly, in good agreement with our parametric measurement. Possible systematics and the accuracy of the TDs and the constraints on the Hubble constant were investigated, for example, by Williams &amp; Liesenborgs (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib79\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib79\">2019</a>) and Grillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib25\">2020</a>). A more accurate determination of the value for the Hubble constant and obtained from an ensemble of models could be made when more accurate measurements of the observed TDs and relative magnifications become available, e.g., exploiting the longer monitoring of SN Refsdal compared to the data in Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>).</p></div></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjac0e32s5\" name=\"apjac0e32s5\">5.\u00a0Summary</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The main purpose of this paper was to present two new, grid-based models for M1149, concentrating on the properties of SN Refsdal: a revised LTM model and a new dPIEeNFW parametric model. The models were built on the same grid, using the same pipeline and with similar inputs, so that a comparison between the two parameterizations is facilitated. No TD or magnification ratio information was used in their minimization.</p><p>We compare our estimates with inferred properties of SN Refsdal and with predictions from other parametric lens models submitted to SN Refsdal's blind prediction challenge as published in Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>) and Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>), and references therein. The TDs and magnification ratios we obtain for SN Refsdal from our parametric, dPIEeNFW model seem to be in very good agreement (to within 1<em>\u03c3</em>\u20132<em>\u03c3</em> typically) with those from most other parametric models, as well as with the early measurements by Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>), Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>), Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>) and with the recent measurements by Baklanov et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib4\">2021</a>). Adopting the measurements for the SX-S1 TD by the latter and simply rescaling the model TD predictions accordingly, the approximate Hubble constant implied by our new parametric model is \u224367 \u00b1 10 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, where the error encompasses the typical range of values from our parametric trial models. This approximate value should be revised when refined measurements of the TDs and magnification ratios become available, and using the full information available when reconstructing the model.</p><p>While the TD predictions from the parametric method are in better agreement with those from most other parametric methods (and especially the models by Grillo and Oguri), they can differ more substantially from the LTM estimates. The TDs with respect to S1, especially for S2, S3, and SX, are systematically lower in the LTM model compared to the parametric model. In addition, the magnification ratios implied by the LTM model, with respect to S1, are generally smaller compared to the parametric technique. In particular, we note that the SX-S1 TD seems to be significantly shorter in the LTM case, which probably underestimates the true reappearance time and leads to a similarly underestimated (and probably unrealistic) lower value for the Hubble constant.</p><p>We compare the mass distributions, magnification maps, and TD surfaces from the two models, and speculate on factors that may lead to the different TD estimates, such as role of individual galaxies and their representation, the representation of the DM component and its internal ellipticity versus external shear or a massive substructure, and whether the accuracy of the model is necessarily an indicator for the correctness of its TD prediction. Updated measurements of SN Refsdal could help decipher between the two solutions more strongly, although it seems that\u2014most notably for SX-S1\u2014the parametric models give more accurate and realistic estimates.</p><p>Most lensing models rely solely on the position of multiple images as constraints. Refined TD and magnification ratio measurements for SN Refsdal should allow to incorporate additional information in the lens models, and break some of the degeneracies inherent to common lensing analyses, and thus, potentially, improve the constraints on the underlying matter distribution of the lens, including the intrinsic shape of the unseen, DM component.</p></div><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>This work is a merged version of two arXiv-version drafts (arXiv:<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://arxiv.org/abs/2008.10641\">2008.10641</a> and arXiv:<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://arxiv.org/abs/2008.10642\">2008.10642</a>; Zitrin <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib86\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib86\">2020a</a>, Zitrin <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib87\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib87\">2020b</a>), united during the review and editorial process. I am very grateful for useful comments by the anonymous reviewer, which helped to improve this work. I kindly thank Pat Kelly, Tommaso Treu, and Steve Rodney for useful discussions. I am also grateful for useful discussions and multiple image voting that took place as a community effort in the HFF framework, and to the respective teams that submitted models\u2014led by PIs Brada\u010d, Natarajan &amp; Kneib (CATS), Merten &amp; Zitrin, Sharon, Williams, Keeton, Bernstein, and Diego, and the GLAFIC group. The work uses some scripts from the astronomy <span class=\"small-caps\">Matlab</span> package (Ofek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib57\">2014</a>). This work is based on observations obtained with the NASA/ESA Hubble Space Telescope, retrieved from the Mikulski Archive for Space Telescopes (MAST) at the Space Telescope Science Institute (STScI). STScI is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc. under NASA contract NAS 5-26555. This research was supported in part by the Ministry of Science &amp; Technology, Israel.</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjac0e32app1\" name=\"apjac0e32app1\">Appendix A: Comparison with the Older LTM Model</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Differences between our revised LTM model and our older LTM models that appeared in Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>), Rodney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>), and Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>) can be divided into three main categories: differences in the input and in the constraints that were used, minor corrections to the code, and a fix to the TD calculation part.</p><p>One difference in input between the older model and the current one, although perhaps not very significant, is that we rerun the photometry of cluster members to create a new member galaxy catalog. Some minor differences exist also for the constraints: in the previous model all multiple image families in the gold list were used except system 14, and a partial list of knots of the spiral galaxy were used, whereas here we use the full gold list (with few silver images in gold systems) and including the full list of knots from Finney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib20\">2018</a>, see also Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>). Systems with no spectroscopic redshift were previously left to be freely optimized in the old model, whereas here these systems (namely, systems 6, and 7) were kept fixed on their typical best-fit photometric redshift, <em>z</em> \u2243 2.6. Image position uncertainties were adopted to be 0.5'' for most systems also in the older model, and for the four SN images for which 0.15'' was used previously, we now used 0.1''.</p><p>One technical difference between the older model and the current one is that the efficiency of the code was slightly improved, so it could be actually run in the native HST resolution whereas before it was typically run with a few times lower resolution and then interpolated to the native pixel scale (or with higher resolution but less MC steps).</p><p>Several other minor corrections to the code have taken place since our 2014 model, but there was no significant change to the modeling scheme. The main improvement in the current model estimates, however, is the fix in the TD calculation part. Thanks to the blind comparison undertaken for SN Refsdal (Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>), we detected a problem in the previously submitted TD surface: the SN images did not seem to form at the extrema of the Fermat surface as expected (see Figure 8 in Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>, Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f7\">7</a> left). After investigating the origin of the problem, we reached the following conclusion\u2014and corresponding fix. In the LTM formalism many of the operations are performed in Fourier space for speed-up purposes. For example, the deflection fields are calculated in Fourier space, and since in our scheme the potential cannot be calculated analytically or parametrically from the mass map (or deflection field), it is instead derived by integrating, in Fourier space, the mass-density distribution times the log of the distance to each point (see Equation (51) in Narayan &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib54\">1996</a>). The potential due to the external shear is then added analytically onto the same grid to obtain the total potential. The total potential was then used together with the deflection fields to estimate the TD surface. The problem was that the gradient of this potential resulted in deflection field components (or if differentiated again, kappa maps) that were verified to be overall very similar to the original deflection fields or kappa map that were integrated to begin with, but in fact were not <em>exactly</em> the same (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f6\">6</a>). Dividing the original deflection fields and kappa map by their parallels obtained via the potential, we get that the mean of the division maps is \u22431 to within 1% in all cases, but that about 5%\u201310% of the pixels in each division map are outliers, differing by more than 5% or 10%, respectively. This discrepancy caused the TD surface to deform (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjac0e32f7\">7</a> left; the effect is similar, essentially, to shifting the source position). To fix this we simply override the <em>original</em> deflection field in each step with the deflection field obtained by taking the gradient of the potential. Hence, the potential in each step now constitutes the new starting point, from which all components of the model are then self-consistently deduced. With this the TD surface, <em>including of our older model submitted to the original comparison test</em>, forms where expected with the SN images in its extrema.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjac0e32f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjac0e32f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=RJ6DiD4ZKZPzbKvxrYtgZjrA4Ec%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=RJ6DiD4ZKZPzbKvxrYtgZjrA4Ec%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0Residual from the calculation of the numerically integrated potential. Right: log<sub>10</sub> of the absolute value of the ratio of the two deflection fields. While the ratio equals unity in most of the field, a numerical residual is clearly seen. Left: example of the deformed TD surface with the old code in which the potential and deflection fields were not perfectly self-consistent (potential obtained by numerical integration). Right: TD surface for the same old model, after correction to the code (the deflection field was taken directly to be the gradient of the (integrated) potential, see text). Red circles mark the positions of the four SN images of the Einstein cross. The TD surface from our new model presented in this work is shown in Figure <a href=\"#apjac0e32f1\">1</a> and the relevant values are listed in Table <a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t1\">1</a>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjac0e32f7\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f7_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=enjVl090Flawg55OXIORkkD%2BD%2FE%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjac0e32f7\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/919/1/54/revision1/apjac0e32f7_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636843391&amp;Signature=jvq5SPmdMXC4ig4w8sOGr%2FJWNHo%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>It should also be mentioned that in retrospect, the error could have been in principle found also without SN Refsdal, just by examining the TD surface on small scales or with sufficiently detailed self-consistency tests. In practice, however, it was the blind comparison and more importantly the fact that our older results for the Einstein cross (i.e., on small scales especially; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>) were outliers that made us notice this issue, eventually.</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjac0e32app2\" name=\"apjac0e32app2\">Appendix B: Multiple Image Constraints</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We list here the constraints used for the lens modeling. The multiple image constraints we use are given in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t2\">2</a>. As mentioned previously, these consist mainly of the gold multiply imaged systems presented in Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>)  and Finney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib20\">2018</a>), with some silver  counter images that our model predicts as secure. We also adopt the detailed mapping of the main, lensed spiral galaxy's internal knots as presented in Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>, and references therein) and Finney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib20\">2018</a>), and use these as additional constraints, as listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjac0e32t3\">3</a>. All systems were fixed to their spectroscopic redshift where available. Systems 6 and 7 do not have a spectroscopic measurement and their redshift was fixed to z\u00a0\u223c\u00a02.6 based on their photo-z. We do not use any measured TDs or magnification ratios as input.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjac0e32t2\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 2.</b>\u00a0Multiple Image align=\"center\">Gold</td></tr></tbody></table><p><small><p>\n                     <strong>Note.</strong> Multiple images used as constraints in the modeling. These systems comprise the gold sample, as defined in Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>) following a ranking of the images based on votes from various lensing teams. While some differences exist, generally speaking, these systems were used in constructing the models for the comparison work of SN Refsdal (Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib39\">2016</a>; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib70\">2016</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>), but some also used the silver sample, not shown in full here. Many of the images in the table (namely, those of systems 1\u22127, and 13) were originally found in our earlier work by previous versions of the LTM model for this cluster (Zitrin &amp; Broadhurst <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib88\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib88\">2009</a>; Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib84\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib84\">2012</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib90\">2015</a>, see also Smith et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib73\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib73\">2009</a>). Indices, coordinates, and redshifts are taken from Finney et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib20\">2018</a>), following Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjac0e32bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjac0e32bib76\">2016</a>, redshifts are mostly based on Grillo et al. <a <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> manuscript Link -->\n<!-- End Accepted manuscript Link -->\n<!-- Start Annotations -->\n <!-- End Annotations --><div class=\"col-no-break wd-jnl-aas-keywords\">\n\t\t<div class=\"replica-h4\">Keywords</div>\n\t\t<p><a href=\"http://astrothesaurus.org/uat/353\">Dark matter</a>; <a href=\"http://astrothesaurus.org/uat/343\">Cosmology</a>; <a gravitational lensing</a>; <a href=\"http://astrothesaurus.org/uat/339\">Cosmological parameters</a>; <a href=\"http://astrothesaurus.org/uat/758\">Hubble constant</a></p>\n\t</div>\n<!-- Start
